[26/03/2025, 12:44:14] Tarquin Leadbetter: Key Points
	•	Research suggests that about 65% of margarita consumers in the UK are women, based on 2017 data.
	•	It seems likely that the age group for these consumers is between 21 and 39, given trends in cocktail preferences.
	•	The evidence leans toward younger adults and women being more inclined to drink cocktails, including margaritas.
Gender Distribution
Based on a 2017 survey, approximately 65% of margarita consumers in the UK are women, indicating a strong preference among female drinkers for this cocktail. This data highlights a gender skew, but more recent figures are not available to confirm if this trend persists.
Age Demographics
While specific data for margarita consumers is limited, general trends suggest that cocktail drinkers, including those favoring margaritas, are often aged between 21 and 39. This age group is more likely to cite cocktails as their beverage of choice, reflecting a younger demographic’s preference for mixed drinks.
Unexpected Detail: Growing Tequila Popularity
An interesting trend is the growing consumption of tequila in the UK, which is a key ingredient in margaritas. This growth, particularly in premium varieties, may be driving increased interest among younger, higher-earning consumers, potentially influencing margarita popularity.

Survey Note: Detailed Analysis of Margarita Consumer Demographics in the UK
This analysis delves into the demographic statistics of margarita cocktail consumers in the UK, drawing on available data from surveys, market reports, and general alcohol consumption trends. The findings aim to provide a comprehensive overview, acknowledging the limitations of outdated or incomplete data.
Gender Profile
The most recent specific data on margarita consumption by gender comes from a 2017 Statista survey, which indicates that 65% of margarita consumers in the UK are women. This suggests a significant gender preference, with women favoring margaritas over men. The survey, conducted via an online method with 1,000 respondents, focused on cocktail drinkers and provides a snapshot of gender distribution for various cocktails, including margaritas Cocktails gender profile in the UK. However, this data is from 2017, and without more recent studies, it is unclear if this proportion has shifted. The interpretation here is that for margaritas, 65% of consumers are women, implying 35% are men, based on the survey’s gender profile context.
Age Demographics
Specific age data for margarita consumers is scarce, but broader trends in cocktail consumption offer insights. An article from 2023, “A Guide to UK Cocktail Consumption,” cites the Statista Global Consumer Survey, noting that individuals aged 21 to 39 are more likely to prefer cocktails and long drinks as their beverage of choice A Guide to UK Cocktail Consumption. This suggests that margarita consumers are likely within this age range, aligning with younger adults’ preferences for mixed drinks. Additionally, a guide on UK pub customer demographics from 2017 highlights that 18-25-year-olds, particularly those single and without children, enjoy spirits and cocktails, further supporting a younger demographic trend UK Pub Customer Demographics & Behaviours. General alcohol consumption data from Drinkaware in 2023 shows that adults aged 18-34 are less likely to drink at low risk levels, potentially indicating higher cocktail consumption among this group, though not specific to margaritas Alcohol Consumption UK.
Income and Other Demographics
Data on income levels or other demographics such as occupation or region for margarita consumers specifically is not readily available. However, trends in tequila consumption, a key component of margaritas, suggest growth in premium segments, which may appeal to higher-earning consumers. For instance, a 2022 report noted a 94% increase in tequila value sales in UK supermarkets, with consumers willing to pay more for premium products, potentially indicating a higher-income demographic Tequila sales up 94% in two years. Regionally, an independent study mentioned in the cocktail guide suggests Edinburgh, Belfast, Bristol, and London have high cocktail consumption, but this is general and not specific to margaritas A Guide to UK Cocktail Consumption.
Trends and Contextual Factors
The popularity of margaritas in the UK is supported by recent reports, such as a 2023 article naming it the UK’s most popular cocktail based on search volume, with 83,750 monthly searches, indicating sustained interest Margarita cocktail named the UK’s most popular. Tequila consumption, crucial for margaritas, is projected to grow by 4.23% from 2024 to 2028, with a market volume reaching US$409.80m, driven by consumer trends like cocktail making at home Tequila - United Kingdom. This growth, particularly in on-trade and off-trade sectors, suggests margaritas remain relevant, especially among younger demographics exploring cocktail culture.
Limitations and Assumptions
It is important to note that the primary data on gender (65% women) is from 2017, and no more recent specific surveys for margarita consumers were found. The age range (21-39) is inferred from general cocktail preference data, which may not be precise for margaritas alone. Assumptions about income and region are based on related trends in tequila and cocktail consumption, but lack direct evidence. The analysis relies on interpreting survey notes and market reports, acknowledging potential changes in consumer behavior over time, especially given the current date of March 26, 2025, and the lack of data post-2023 in accessible sources.

In conclusion, while the data suggests margarita consumers in the UK are primarily women aged 21-39, with potential higher income levels and regional variations, the reliance on older data (2017) and general trends necessitates caution. Future studies could provide more precise insights into this demographic, especially given the growing interest in tequila and cocktails as of 2025.

[31/03/2025, 15:00:34] Max Enemigo: Just on this- MMI and A+E are actually owned by the same sheik at the top. You have some smaller ones in the Northern Emirates, and since Dubai (re)introduced the tax on Alcohol sales, people do now go out there to buy again (Barracuda is the biggest) but they don’t do on premise directly (they sell to A+E/MMI). So it depends if your brand is on-premise or off-premise focussed. For on, it’s only MMI and A+E, and as mentioned they are literally a different side of the same coin. There are, let’s say politely, challenges when working with either. ‎<This message was edited>

Qland all venues keep the 10% to buy a few champagne and rare spirits.
This being said, if you sell bitters. You shouldn’t stress too much over this

[04/04/2025, 10:21:59] Jack Orr Ewing: Bag in Box question:
We're getting quotes from Venus for delivering a 10L BiB alongside our bottles
Their margin for delivering 10L (14.3 bottles) is exactly the same as delivering 14.3 bottles
Despite the dimensions of a 10L BiB being the same as a case of 6 x 70cl
And the weight of 10L being 10.3kg vs 19kg for 12 bottles
So half the weight, half the size, same price

Appreciate that they're trying to protect their unit margins and don't want to lose £ by supporting BiBs

Anyone, especially @⁨Tim Dunlop⁩/Sapling / other BiB fanatics - managed to get Venus or any other wholesalers to charge a lower rate for them than equivalent bottles

It’s really good if you happen to use Unleashed - the integration and data analysis etc that you get is incredible… we use it because it’s very B2B focussed, we found a lot of the CRMs were heavily skewed towards new business. 

Not sure what it’s like if you don’t use Unleashed though
[15/04/2025, 14:00:56] Tarquin Leadbetter: Yeah we use that too, with unleashed
[15/04/2025, 14:01:32] Gary Read Bolle Drinks: Depends a lot on what you need I’d say. We use Hubspot extensively because of the ability to get all the marketing and sales and social in a single place. But it’s ££ !
[15/04/2025, 14:05:13] Geoff Watts: My general view is that the usefulness of any CRM system comes down to how you use it and embed it in what you do. Lots of people think issues are system driven (and a lot aren't perfect to be fair) but often the reason the grass looks greener elsewhere is because the current one isn't used as it should be. Sales team don't engage for whatever reason (admin usually, but also the practicality of doing it before/after a call) and they see it as a chore not an information source, then centrally the team don't get the information they want from it etc and then you get into a doom loop of "data is wrong no point using it" "you aren't using it why should I put the data in"
[15/04/2025, 14:06:50] Jack Rogan: I've used Bowimi and for people out doing door to door/field work it was great, simple to use and you could capture what was needed.

Hubspot is pay to play and add on.

I've heard from other people who liked Repsly and moved to other brands that they want repsly back so think that's decent.

Key thing with CRMs is to keep the mantra of 'if it isn't in the CRM is doesn't exist/didn't happen'!
[15/04/2025, 14:06:51] Jack Orr Ewing: 100% agree
We've just started with Bowimi - will feed back once we figure out what's good/bad about it
[15/04/2025, 14:07:12] Jack Rogan: Bowimi also good at trying to make it better

[06/05/2025, 23:41:43] Adam Giraffe: The recently finalized UK-India Free Trade Agreement primarily addresses tariff reductions on British whisky and gin. Under the agreement, India's import duties on these spirits will decrease from 150% to 75% initially, with a further reduction to 40% over a ten-year period. 
While the deal also includes tariff eliminations on UK soft drinks, there is no specific mention of other spirits such as vodka, rum, or liqueurs in the available summaries. Therefore, it appears that the tariff reductions are currently limited to whisky and gin
[06/05/2025, 23:42:08] Adam Giraffe: appears just whiskey and Gin at mo Jack.
[07/05/2025, 06:56:41] Si Wright Burnt Faith: Would be unusual to be only specific categories
[07/05/2025, 06:59:27] Jamie Isenwater: Limiting it to two categories is much more politically acceptable to the domestic audience of India. Gin is small there and the Scotch negotiations have been going on for 20 years…
[07/05/2025, 07:31:54] Si Wright Burnt Faith: Refuse to believe it (says guys exporting brandy to India) 🤣
[07/05/2025, 09:05:43] Paritosh Bhandari: For now, only Gin and Whisky (most likely restricted only to Scotch Whisky).

India reached a similar agreement with the US couple of months back (only Bourbon, not all American Spirits or Whiskies).

Reduction of customs duty is/will be done over a period of time.

Also, as Excise (on Alcohol) is a State subject in India, the States are free to have their own (local) duty structure.

For everyone's knowledge, the Imported Gin market in India may just be about 300K-350K Cases while Scotch alone (fully imported or bulk that is then bottled locally) is nearly 8Million Cases. 

Bulk Scotch that's used in quite a few of Indian whiskies figures is over and above the 8Mil fig.

FYI.

[19/05/2025, 11:16:37] Nick Britton: Question is how thorough/ buy the book you want to be. If the cans will be used just for one event and will be guaranteed to sell within say 1 week, you might be able to skip some steps. One of the things that takes a while is once you've developed your formula is doing accelerated aging tests (12 weeks), to determine a shelf life, and also, can integrity checking.
If you have a great liquid but it's not compatible with your cans, you have an issue.

[19/05/2025, 11:37:05] James Lewis: Tesco quote GS1 in their standards. Will copy some text from their induct platform now
[19/05/2025, 11:38:40] James Lewis: ‘Enter the unique barcode number as displayed on the product packaging.
 
UK/ROI
 
Tesco Sell by weight PLU - the system generates 6 digits followed by 7 zeros (02 prefix)
 
Branded Sell by weight PLU - supplier enters the 7 digits followed by 6 zeros (20 prefix)
 
If your product is delivered as a single unit or direct to store, its Product and Case barcode number will be the same and achieved by adding a leading zero to the Product barcode number E.g. 9781789080544 becomes 09781789080544. Cannot be used for EAN 8 barcode number type.
 
If using a UPC A barcode number type for your product, then you will need to add two leading zeros.
 
If using a EAN 13 barcode number type at case level you will need to add a leading zero, making it 14 digits.
 
CE
 
Tesco Sell by weight PLU - the system generates 6 digits followed by 7 zeros (21, 28 prefix)
 
Branded Sell by weight PLU - supplier enters the 6 digits followed by 7 zeros (29 prefix) e.g. 2912340000000’
[19/05/2025, 11:40:37] James Lewis: Case barcode type is ITF 14
[19/05/2025, 11:47:02] Nick Britton: Can't see anything in here that specifically says it has to be a barcode bought from GS1 though?  Debate is GS1 standard versus bought from GS1.  Analogy would be with my car service, I go to somewhere that is VW approved rather than VW because it's half the price and just as good quality.

7.1. Either Party may terminate this Agreement by giving [30/60] days’ written notice.

7.2. Either Party may terminate immediately if the other Party breaches any material obligation and fails to remedy it within [14] days of notice.

7.3. Upon termination, the Retailer may continue to sell remaining stock but must cease representing any exclusivity.

8. Intellectual Property
The Supplier retains all rights to trademarks, branding, and marketing materials. The Retailer agrees not to modify or misrepresent the Supplier’s branding.

9. Confidentiality
Both Parties agree to maintain the confidentiality of the terms of this Agreement and any non-public information disclosed during the relationship.

10. Miscellaneous
Governing Law: This Agreement shall be governed by the laws of [Jurisdiction].

Entire Agreement: This document constitutes the full agreement between the Parties.

Amendments: Must be in writing and signed by both Parties.

Assignment: Neither Party may assign its rights without prior written consent.

[30/05/2025, 09:40:01] Ed Belgrove: Any kindreds using Quickbooks who want to save on their subscription; I was paying £47+ VAT/month for Quickbooks Plus, I’m now paying £18.80 +VAT/month. (I called QB to ask for a discount and they gave me this solution). Saves £338.40/year.

Firstly set up a free Quickbooks Accountant account (use a different company email address - can just be your general hello@ or info@ company email; then back on your main QB account select ‘Transfer billing to my accountant’ under Subscriptions & Billing and select your newly set up account; your new QB Accountant account can access cheaper pricing on your behalf. You’ll also need to add payment details to the newly set up QB account so payment can be made. 

Thought it was worth sharing. If anyone wants a hand with it just drop me a message.👍🏻

Assume not but... 

This guy at QR Squared keeps badgering me for meetings - and says that GS1 are shutting down barcodes in 2 yrs and moving to QR codes... which means we all need to start changing back labels to QR codes. Apparently:
With QR-Squared, you can:
✅ Create GS1-compliant dynamic QR codes for your bottles, or packaging
✅ Link directly to up-to-date ingredient, allergen, and nutritional info
✅ Update product details anytime — no need to reprint labels
✅ Offer a clean, branded, mobile-friendly experience to your customers
✅ Manage all your products from one easy dashboard

It feels like this might start to be something that's a benefit and if you're printing large runs of labels now, maybe something to thing about. Anyone heard of this and planning to switch?

[11/06/2025, 09:25:11] Alex Wolpert: We do, and we’ve had the same letter, and asked for clarification
[11/06/2025, 09:35:13] Fab Heartcut: Dear Fabrizio,

Thank you for your email and for taking the time to look into the government guidance - I really appreciate you doing that research.

You're absolutely spot on - your business doesn't meet the requirements for direct EPR obligations, and you're quite right that you don't need to register or pay EPR fees directly to the government. We completely agree with your assessment of your situation.

I think where there might be a bit of confusion is around the different types of obligations in the legislation.

What we've found is that the same EPR rules that exempt you also create obligations for us as your packaging supplier. We're required to:

Report all the packaging we supply to the UK market
Pay EPR disposal fees to the government
Manage compliance for all our UK sales
The challenge we've faced is that these costs have to be managed somehow. Rather than building them into our general pricing (which would affect everyone), we've decided to be transparent about these regulatory costs by showing them separately.

So you're absolutely right - you're not paying EPR fees. What's happening is that you're helping us cover the costs we now have to pay because of these new regulations.

I completely understand this feels frustrating, especially when you've correctly identified that the rules don't directly apply to your business.

Would it be helpful if I shared the specific section of the guidance that covers supplier obligations?

Best regards,
[11/06/2025, 09:35:14] Fab Heartcut: Just had this response from them (after me pushing back).

[17/06/2025, 12:35:13] Nick Britton: Depends on vibe looking for.  Ushuaia is a favourite of mine for a scale commercial house.  Fun day.  5pm - Midnight.

If you want to blow the budget, and willing to travel, go to UNVRS tonight.
528 could also be good tonight - pikes presents, probably the best value late night club on the island, drinks a reasonable price and cool outdoor venue (you can also get High Water there...)
For day time fun - O Beach is a fun day out - cheesy, most of TOWIE will be there, but simple fun, about 25 mins in a cab (buy High Water if you go 🙂).
If you want classy dinner, go to Roto other side of the old town, great vibe, good food, pricey but fun.  For day time, as PJ says, Beach House or Nassau if you want to stay local. If you can be bothered to travel (and can get space), Amante is amazing (particularly for dinner).  Then you've got the classics of the likes of blue marlin, 150 euros for a sun bed, but nice tunes and a good view (bit more pretentious vibe).  Sa Trinxa is also nice for a cheapish day at a nice beach with good food and good vibes (10 min taxi there).
Enjoy!

[17/06/2025, 13:03:13] Tatiana Three: This message is for a not too riotous group of posh boys who had never been to ibiza and wanted to experience fun but nothing too hectic…. plus I wrote it last year so may be out of date. Especially since privilege has now reopened as UNVRS and I cannot wait to check it out

 First up, they must have lunch one day at Utopia in San Miguel Port. The Port beach itself is ugly but bear with me - if they walk onto the beach and when they reach the sea turn left up a footpath that leads around the corner of the cliff, they’ll find themselves following the path down into a secluded bay with the sweetest beach restaurant down there. The food is cooked in an open kitchen, they make delicious cocktails there, it’s so off the beaten track and so lovely. Plus they have day beds for sunbathing and it’s a sweet little spot for a swim.

If they want to splash out one day, and for a really good swanky ibiza experience and a bit of celeb spotting, they should go to Chiringuito on Es Cavallet. The food is really good in a lovely white drapey overpriced but worth it setting!

If they want to dance but not brave the super clubs, they should go to Pikes. Freddie Mercury threw a birthday party there once and Wham filmed the video for Club Tropicana there. It’s legendary, and has a great restaurant, a lovely outdoor bar by the pool and then a cool little nightclub at the back. They have lots of different events every night so they should check out the website and choose the one that sounds the most fun and then go go go! https://pikesibiza.com/party/calendar/

One of the evenings, I recommend going into Sta Gertrudis and exploring the shops there and then having drinks in the main square by the church, and then having dinner in the pretty little garden at Macau which is an excellent Italian restaurant. Similarly, an evening shopping in the old town will not dissapoint, and then have a table booked at either El Olivo OR La Oliva (sister restaurants and next door to each other) up in Villa de Dalt. 

If they are feeling brave, the best club to go to is probably Pacha. It’s an institution, and on Wednesdays they do a night called Flower Power where they play ONLY sixties and seventies music. Everyone dresses up in flower headresses and floaty dresses - it is just funny seeing the club that normally plays hardcore dff dff dff dff play epic Beatles songs. I recommend it!

[26/06/2025, 15:03:34] Jack Orr Ewing: Thought this is quite interesting - and I like the phrase "all the negative externalities that come with sobriety"

https://x.com/s8mb/status/1937979132442268043

I'm sure I'm over-sensitive as someone "all in" on alcohol - but I'm keen to start developing a more robust language for defending the industry against this rising anti-booze narrative. There's chatter today about alcohol advertising being banned on TV and Radio pre-9pm (honestly this is probably better for indies who can't afford that anyway, but might push those budgets into "our" channels and make them even less affordable)…

And the WHO view that there is "no safe level" of alcohol is just so misleading - there is a safe level. We know it. It's just that for everyone who goes way beyond that safe level, they got into the swing of things via the safe level... the "bathwater" here is the millions of people being priced and scared out of moderate drinking

[09/07/2025, 13:32:18] Jack Orr Ewing: Hot take... vanity matters
I'm fairly sure that whether you do 10,000 samples outside Oxford Circus or 0, your rate of sale in national and London based accounts will be the same, and you may sell 1 or 2 more bottles via your website
Same for whether you post 3 times a week or twice, and whether you get 100 likes or 300 likes per post
Distribution is the primary driver of growth
And vanity metrics influence distribution decisions
So do stuff that makes you look like a big brand doing cool shit (and try not to spend too much doing it)

[09/07/2025, 16:15:47] Caner Veli: All that matters is C x F x AOV: 
Customers
Frequency
Average Order Value

Everything else is upstream from this. Measure Sell through for on-trade and off-trade
Aim for conversion rates (3-5%) and AOP for DTC. 
Aim for Overall Marketing Efficiency Ratio  1:20

Ahead of conversions on paid ads benchmarks are:
CPM £10-18
CTR 1-2% with 5% being great depending on channel. 

The more customers you speak to the better your understanding of them and how your product resonates. Amplify their feedback and content via video reviews. 
Remember to sell the ‘dream vacation’ / superpower vs some features/ liquid.

[16/07/2025, 05:55:23] Matthew De Jesus: Morning all! I wondered if you could help guide our thinking/decision making here; what’s the minimum gross margin you would sell to a wholesaler at before you walk away?
[16/07/2025, 06:24:07] Geoff Watts: Depends on a lot of things to be honest. What volume are they doing? Does it open up crucial on trade partners, do those on trade partners need further support? Could the price give you problems in the market if other wholesalers get wind of it/can’t compete with the outward price that results
[16/07/2025, 06:44:27] Matthew De Jesus: Volumes - TBC, depends how successful they are but they will pre buy a decent amount. Also TBC if this is opening up crucial trade partners. However, it will open up a brand new market/region where we don’t sell today. Pricing won't give issue with other wholesalers and market to our knowledge so far
[16/07/2025, 06:51:20] Robert Darfi: We standardise wholesale pricing, everyone pays the same. Let them scrap it out themselves. We then support at retail level and work closely with the wholesalers on this.

If you do sell to wholesalers at wildly different prices, in my opinion it’ll tend to come back and bite you. They all buy each other, buyers move between them etc.

They’re incredibly difficult to focus too. So if you think selling at a sharp price will automatically get you traction, you might be disappointed.

That said… if a wholesaler came to me with an absolute ton of guaranteed margin. Then there’s a discussion to be had!

Personal opinion anyway.
[16/07/2025, 07:01:19] Tim Dunlop: Yep secondary wholesaling is a big market. Like Rob says everyone knows everyone and pallets of stock move between companies for relatively small margin if one has a better price that the other. Flat pricing is the best way to go and then build JBP’s with key partners to invest in their business to engage with customers and sales teams to drive rate of sale
[16/07/2025, 07:07:48] ~ Andy Newcombe: Thanks for sharing this.

[21/07/2025, 15:22:40] Tarquin Leadbetter: *Duty Stamps* in theory, is old UK duty stamped stock fine to export now? Without needing to destroy/cover stamps?
[21/07/2025, 15:25:19] Gary Read Bolle Drinks: We attended last year. Awful. Virtually zero leads despite us winning Best of Show.
[21/07/2025, 15:26:13] Jamie Baxter: Yes it is ok. From 1 May 2025 you no longer need to follow the rules for removing or obliterating stamps on product for export. If you wish to remove stamps you may do so at your discretion and without notifying HMRC.

This also applies if the exported products are duty paid and you intend to make a drawback claim. However, you must still meet all the other requirements and conditions for drawback as described in Excise duty drawback (Excise Notice 207).

[24/07/2025, 18:14:46] Dom Richardson: 🇬🇧➡️🇮🇳 UK–India Trade Deal: Spirits Tariff Summary

Under the new UK–India trade deal, Indian customs duties on UK spirits are being reduced — but only if you meet the Minimum Import Price (MIP).

🥃 For liqueurs and most other spirits (vodka, rum, brandy, tequila, etc.):

MIP: USD $5 per litre or USD $6 per 750ml bottle

Customs duty:

Year 1: 110%

Reduces each year

Year 10 onwards: 75%


❌ If MIP isn’t met: full 150% duty still applies


🍸 For gin and whisky:

No MIP mentioned

Customs duty:

Starts at 75%

Reduces to 40% by Year 10



📦 Bottles other than 750ml or 1L aren’t mentioned, so it's unclear how those will be assessed under the MIP rules.

[28/07/2025, 20:16:00] Varun Sarna: hi guys - should you include the cost of your bulk shipping cartons in COGS? ‎<This message was edited>

[28/07/2025, 20:18:35] Caner Veli: I tend to. I also include payment processing and fulfilment costs.
[28/07/2025, 20:19:59] Varun Sarna: yeah i would have thought so

chat gpt seems to suggests there's a difference if the consumer never sees it - so if it's delivered to the on-trade, yes - if it's delivered to amazon and then repacked into single bottle cartons, then no
[28/07/2025, 20:21:52] Jamie Isenwater: If it’s not in COGS, where do you put it then?!
[28/07/2025, 20:23:18] Varun Sarna: overheads apparently: 

"categorize them as a fulfilment or logistics expense, part of operating expenses (OPEX)

Why?
COGS should include only the direct costs of producing the product that ends up with the customer, like:

Bottle
Label
Liquid
Tamper seal
Retail box (if there is one)

But the outer shipping carton to Amazon is part of the logistics cost of moving product, not making it. Amazon might even specify certain packaging requirements (e.g. FBA prep), but this still falls under distribution, not production."
[28/07/2025, 20:25:13] Jamie Isenwater: Whatever costs are needed to get the product to your customer (ie Amazon) are COGS
[28/07/2025, 20:27:51] Varun Sarna: right, anyone able to give a ballpark of cost of custom/branded cartons at scale (per bottle)? 

£0.05-0.10 is what I have down
[28/07/2025, 20:31:48] Geoff Watts: Shows what ChatGPT knows then… useful tool but not infallible. Cases are 100% cogs.  As frankly are the logistics costs for delivery to Amazon.
[28/07/2025, 20:39:59] Tarquin Leadbetter: Depends if you want them all thick double walled courier compatible (if supplying a portion of customers by the case via fedex etc) 10-15p bottle. Or thin for pallets deliveries only 5p - 10p. Volume dependent.
[28/07/2025, 20:46:46] Tim Dunlop: Personally COGS is everything needed to get a finished product (bottle, cork, closure, liquid, label and outer case). Shipping cost to customer (Amazon or wholesaler) doesn’t get included into COGS and should be factored into distribution / logistics
[28/07/2025, 20:54:04] Varun Sarna: I've seen models that work it that way. Hence my confusion.

In the wholesale situation Tim are you thinking when selling ex works?
[28/07/2025, 21:03:43] Robert Darfi: We’re the same. COGS is bottle, liquid, label, closure and cap (aka unit / each). We would also class the inner as COGS.

We draw the line at the outer (although the definition of what an outer is depends on pack format I guess).

Couple of examples. We have a 6x70cl pack format. With this we class the bottle, label, liquid etc and box as COGS.

We also have a 1.5ltr BiB format. With this we class the BiB, box and liquid as COGS. We transport them in 4x1.5ltr outers which we don’t class as COGS.

Shipping / distribution costs we certainly wouldn’t class as COGS.
[28/07/2025, 21:04:05] Tim Dunlop: So in all sectors I work on COGS being finished product delivered to London City Bond in the format we sell on (so 6x70cl for bottles, single 5 litres for BiB etc). Everything after that comes under distribution. ‎<This message was edited>

[31/07/2025, 08:08:18] Geoff Watts: Re complexity aim to keep it as simple as possible when you start so you can focus resource on other things but with an eye on where you might want to head and so give yourself long term flexibility. ‎[31/07/2025, 08:10:08] Adam Giraffe: Hi Andy. All handling businesses within the process  have to be organic approved and certified and the importer needs to be certified for importation too ( additional audit we understand), COI (cert of inspection) this has to be raised prior to departure and then sent to the port for health authorisation prior to goods getting to port, for this you'll need Organic pre-notification form, copy of COI,  Commercial docs (invoice packing list COO etc), transport docs (waybill / bill of landing etc). 

Hope this helps ‎image omitted
[31/07/2025, 08:12:11] Adam Giraffe: if outside of EU it may vary.
[31/07/2025, 08:17:42] ~ Andy Newcombe: From France
[31/07/2025, 08:18:47] ~ Andy Newcombe: This looks great - product is coming from France
[31/07/2025, 08:23:06] Adam Giraffe: if you follow that you'll be fine.  Just make sure everyone is certified correctly or else its a pain and costly to recertify as Organic once its imported (send back and re-import). All you should need to do as a receiver is sign the Pre notification form as the departure location should raise the paperwork and send it to the port authority.

A friend has 6 of the 400mm wide x 2m high stands (hardware only in carry cases) that I’m going to put on eBay, but thought I’d mention to you incase you know of anyone that can use them. They are really neat and great for impact in small spaces. Much sturdier than bannerstands and can be updated with new graphics (its a fabric ’sock’ that pulled over the top and zips at the base, can be double sided or single sided print) They are also magnetic so you can link together if needed. 
Some have never been used and a couple used only once. looking to get £50 each for them which is a bargain!
There are lots of companies that will print the replacement socks

[27/08/2025, 09:28:10] Tarquin Leadbetter: I would assume all supermarkets have CHEP accounts
[27/08/2025, 09:28:44] Tarquin Leadbetter: But… I’d try not to be put on CHEPS if you can avoid.
[27/08/2025, 09:29:08] Tarquin Leadbetter: Sometimes we’ve had to pay to re-work on to non CHEPS pallets
[27/08/2025, 09:29:14] Tarquin Leadbetter: To avoid the hassle
[27/08/2025, 09:29:43] Tarquin Leadbetter: But there might be some CHEPS experts and advocates in here!
[27/08/2025, 09:33:05] Nick Britton: After 5 years, we've just come off Chep! We thought we had to be on them for customers, but as long as you're using good quality pallets, you don't need them and it's a pain in the arse. You will get fined if you just dump them and it becomes expensive. They also charge you £35 to receive a pallet somewhere if it's not an 'automatic' chep address (eg Amazon, where they don't check). Avoid it if you can.

[09/09/2025, 10:30:46] Gary Ford Forged: just had push back from someone selling into Tesco stating abv needs to be on the rear of a bottle along with volume. I am of the understanding they both need to be on the bottle and in the same field of vision. And that just being on the front is ok. views?
[09/09/2025, 11:00:05] Jamie Baxter: Legally the abv must be in the same field of vision as the bottle volume and the legal name of the product. Just to be clear, that’s the legal name ie if you are making a spiced rum, the legal name is something like “rum based spirit drink” and not spiced rum which most brand owners would prefer to be on the back. Tesco of course might have a policy that goes further than the legal requirement

[09/09/2025, 12:46:13] Jamie Baxter: That particular Captain Morgan product is, I believe, a rum ie no flavourings. European legislation is clear that rum shall not be flavoured. If you look at their “spiced rum” from memory it says nothing on the front label when it comes to product name - the brand is big enough so everyone knows its a spiced rum. On the back label it has the legal name ie “rum based spirit drink” or similar which gets around the legal problem. The knock on from that is that the abv and bottle size must be on the back label in same field of vision as legal name (it can be repeated on front if wished). I don’t know the Kraken bottle well enough to know if they’ve done the same
[09/09/2025, 12:49:39] Jamie Baxter: There are many examples of people labelling products incorrectly. The Gin Guild has done fantastic work getting products, especially low alcohol “gins” relabelled since gin must be over 37.5%abv. There’s no body quite so exercised about rum and similar products just yet

[15/09/2025, 17:08:47] Stuart Coleman: Hi everyone. Quick note from me:

Sad to announce that this year’s DRINK IT IN Festival will not be going ahead. With eight weeks to go, I've not been able to secure the number of brand commitments we required to make the event viable. Through conversations with many fantastic producers, it has become clear that marketing budgets are under increased pressure across the industry, making it especially challenging to achieve the necessary level of support this year. My vision was always to create a platform where the trade and consumers alike can discover a broad range of high-quality, independent UK spirits in an engaging and relaxed environment. Without a sufficient critical mass of brands, I couldn't deliver the experience I  set out to achieve, and believe it is the right decision to postpone.

[17/09/2025, 12:29:30] Nick Britton: Key watch out on LCBO is shipping! I explored it but couldn't make value chain work. They insist on their shipping which is approx x2 going rate. They then add their margin on to the landed cost including their over inflated shipping. Your price in retail likely to be aroubd 1.5 to x 2 your price in UK. And for less margin. Potentially big volume but consumer has to really want a product a lot more than a local version for about half the price....
[17/09/2025, 12:57:36] Adam Giraffe: We have a couple of customers who ship to LCBO but its order only as far I make out (not our relationship), LCBO place an  order that has to be on the water by such a date, stocks gets sent via external logistics by the agreed date ( the customers use different logistic partners so think there is a way around using LCBO shipping).

[03/10/2025, 10:06:15] Jamie Baxter: ANNEX XII

ALCOHOLIC STRENGTH

The actual alcoholic strength by volume of beverages containing more than 1,2 % by volume of alcohol shall be indicated by a figure to not more than one decimal place. It shall be followed by the symbol ‘% vol.’ and may be preceded by the word ‘alcohol’ or the abbreviation ‘alc’.
The alcoholic strength shall be determined at 20 °C.

[17/10/2025, 11:51:11] Lloyd White: I have done it as buyer and also as a supplier

From a buyers perspective I liked it as I saw 20 people in a day, I knew who I was seeing and what (as a business) we needed to do. On the back on the event I did years ago we listed 4 products from the event from food to drink. We liked it, as seeing the potential 'pool' would have been days of meetings as opposed to one day. Additionally some items we would have dismissed from info on email or the web, until you actually see them we would have missed the opportunity.

As an exhibitor I have done it, and whilst pricey it only takes one listing in theory to get pay back. From the last one in early 2024 we obtained two listings - WH Smith and also Warner hotels. It ultimately depends on the confirmed buyers list who are attending (Atom pay the costs for the buyers to be there and stay overnight etc..)

[19/10/2025, 20:36:56] Tarquin Leadbetter: Delivery note would be a packing slip / packing list from your accounting or inventory software (like your invoice, but without prices on it). Can just attached to the pallet inside a documents pouch. The haulier company (or courier) will get a proof of delivery themselves with a signature (which you can use as a POD if needed) ‎<This message was edited>

Prior to that I didn’t use anything 🤯… I remember panic, after losing one of my carbon copy books that hadn’t been invoiced yet 😅 ‎<This message was edited>

[11/11/2025, 20:33:48] Max Enemigo: Quick one for the group on exit tax proposals: https://www.no-exit-tax.co.uk
Those of us in spirits already carry one of the heaviest tax burdens in UK business. Duty, employment costs, corporation tax, VAT on every bottle. We’ve absorbed it all because we back ourselves and believe in building here.
But the proposed exit tax on unrealised gains and further CGT increases with reduced BADR relief fundamentally changes the calculation. It’s not about avoiding tax on actual gains, it’s about being penalised for taking entrepreneurial risk in the first place.
Why build a business in the UK when comparable jurisdictions actively incentivise risk-taking rather than punishing it? This isn’t about wanting a free ride, it’s about basic competitive sense. The policy is economically illiterate and will drive exactly the businesses and founders the country needs to retain.

Worth signing if you agree. End of rant!
[11/11/2025, 20:36:39] Jack Orr Ewing: I’m much more worried about the noises coming out of the treasury about another big duty hike…
[11/11/2025, 20:38:30] Max Enemigo: I’ve missed this?
[11/11/2025, 20:43:41] Si Wright Burnt Faith: I think this is probably one of the lesser important issue amongst the wider issues faced in hospo. If you get to this point you’ve probably won already.
[11/11/2025, 20:55:47] Max Enemigo: The principal of taxing an unrealised gain is terrifying- if you have a UK company, and it makes say 50k a year in profit or has inventory of 50k, or has raised at a valuation of 100k and you own 50% still it would theoretically be taxed at 20% if you decided to move to any other country in the world for whatever reason. That’s 10k, even if you apply BADR which is not guaranteed it would be 5k. Arguably if you’ve already won, and cashed out a large sum, it actually won’t feel as bad as it would for those without ‘the broadest shoulders’.

[28/11/2025, 14:11:37] Adam Giraffe: Any UK address can be used even be your accountants addresss! Just used a contact point to be traceable. Like Tim said only recently changed.  There is Gov link that sets its all out, I'll try to find it when back at desk.

[03/02/2026, 05:45:52] Paritosh Bhandari: Quick check:

As a (broad) rule of thumb, what's the Landing to a Multiple/Wholesale (Tesco or Holland & Barrett, for ex) for a Functional Beverage (non-alc/non carbonated) if the RRP is £2/Bottle?

To ascertain this chain: 

Landing to Wholesale <> W/S Margin <> Billing to Retail <> Retailer Margin <> VAT <> RRP

[26/02/2026, 12:33:56] Damola Timeyin: On the point of the mega brands. It’s worth reading the Diageo earnings call from yesterday
[26/02/2026, 12:34:14] Damola Timeyin: https://seekingalpha.com/article/4874401-diageo-plc-deo-q2-2026-earnings-call-prepared-remarks-transcript

the transcript for anyone interested
[26/02/2026, 12:36:35] Damola Timeyin: the TLDR:

- Diageo H1 FY2026 results: organic net sales and operating profit both down 2.8%
- North America down ~7% organically described as faster deterioration in US spirits affordability than expected
- Tequila down 23% organically in North America Don Julio and Casamigos both declined significantly
- Dave Lewis (7 weeks in) said Diageo has under-invested in customer relationships and that 65% of global customer orders are entered manually
- Diageo cutting dividend and redirecting capital to invest in portfolio competitiveness and balance sheet
- Europe, Latin America, and Africa were the growth bright spots
- Diageo flagged their portfolio is underrepresented in higher-volume price tiers and is considering "surgical" price repositioning by brand, pack size, and channel
- Full strategy review underway, update expected calendar Q2 2026
[26/02/2026, 12:38:13] Damola Timeyin: Things to consider according to claude, if you don’t want to go through it all.

- US spirits affordability is declining, pricing strategy and value communication matters more now
- Tequila's collapse creates a shelf and mind-space opportunity, buyers are actively looking for what’s next
- Diageo is going to get more aggressive in mid-premium pricing,  expect more competition in that tier
- Big players are cutting dividends and tightening belts, distributors may be more open to indie brands that offer margin and excitement
- Diageo admitted poor trade execution, an opportunity to win on service, responsiveness, and relationship quality
- Africa is a growth market globally,  lean into provenance storytelling, the continent has commercial credibility right now

[10/03/2026, 12:39:17] Penny Polar: FBA is great, but I’ve had some issues. Amazon kept losing inventory when I started, then I started recording everything I sent in, this seems to have permanently resolved the issue. 
Around christmas I started getting a lot of refunds due to breakages. Couldn’t work out why until a customer posted an unwrapping video and they were sending out bottles in paper bags 🤦🏼‍♀️ 
I’d still use FBA every time but you need to keep a close eye on  amazon and if something isn’t adding up get straight onto their customer service (which I think has improved a bit recently). Happy to chat further!

[12/03/2026, 22:28:55] Max Enemigo: My reply, In case anyone else would like a idea is:
[12/03/2026, 22:30:33] Max Enemigo: Hi Jay,
Thank you for the notice, received and noted.
That said, I have a number of concerns with both the substance and the manner of this surcharge.
A 10% fuel surcharge, applied against a 7.3% increase in fuel pricing over the two weeks since the Iranian conflict broke out, appears excessive from the outset. The surcharge exceeds the underlying cost movement, which raises an obvious question as to how the figure was arrived at.
Equally concerning is the timing. Introducing the increase after the cut-off for Friday deliveries, thereby removing any opportunity for customers to move product ahead of the surcharge, feels less like an operational necessity and more like a deliberate choice.
It is worth noting that the energy and fuel companies are currently under investigation by the CMA, whose preliminary view is that recent pricing does not reflect genuine cost pressure and amounts to profiteering.
At a time of immense pressure across the drinks industry, this has not landed well, and judging by the feedback I have received, that sentiment is widely shared among LCB's customer base. The lack of prior warning, the timing designed to prevent any pre-surcharge movement, and the rounding up to a clean 10% all sit uncomfortably with the assertion that there is no element of profiteering at play.
We would welcome a more transparent breakdown of how this figure was calculated, and a conversation about how LCB intends to manage this fairly going forward.
Best,

[13/03/2026, 10:36:44] Max Enemigo: Dear Max

 

I refer to your e-mail below in reply to our notification that we will be implementing a fuel surcharge of 10% from orders delivered form the 16th March.

 

I am not sure where the figure you have quoted of a 7.3% increase is based on. As I am sure you will appreciate the hauliers we work with generally buy fuel in bulk and not at the pumps, the increases they are quoting are between 30 to 35%, from their last order to their current order. I attach some of the correspondence received from our hauliers and you will see that most of the increases have already been implemented and we have suffered this increase before having no option other than to pass this charge on.

 

Whilst the matter is being investigated by the CMA it has not prevented the cost from increasing and our hauliers must purchase the fuel at these inflated charges to continue trading and delivering goods.

 

We are part of the drinks industry and we are facing the same pressure as our customers and to imply that we are profiteering is offensive as we have attempted to delay the increase as long as possible. If you are able to receive orders from your customers today for delivery on Monday we will not charge the fuel surcharge on those orders.

 

The 10% fuel surcharge will be shown at the bottom of the invoice and will be on the total of all the deliveries made.

 

We hope as I am sure everybody does that the matter in Iran will be resolved soon and that fuel costs will reduce back and we can remove/reduce the fuel surcharge.

 

Best regards

 

Michael Stone

DIRECTOR

Alongside Tequila Enemigo I founded a strategic advisory out of the Middle East with a few ex-S&P people in 2024, and wrote something yesterday trying to translate what we're tracking into practical terms for indie spirits brands - shipping, distributor dynamics, contract terms, where the credit risk actually sits. [09/04/2026, 21:05:09] ~ Josh Powell: Get some small bottles that you can top up 50ml and leave with people after you've done a proper tasting after visiting potential accounts
[09/04/2026, 21:06:00] ~ Adam Willmore: Beet me to it!
[09/04/2026, 21:15:11] Robert Darfi: Completely depends on the customer & situation for me.

If it’s a formalised meeting you can probably sample a single serve. I keep open bottles in my car for this purpose.

If I’m leaving samples behind then it depends on the potential opportunity. Generally, smaller volumes - a bottle. Mid size opportunities - a few mixed bottles. Large opportunities - I’ve dropped hundreds of pounds worth of stock into before / full bottles of every SKU etc.

That’s the way I do it, others I’m sure would be different. There’s no ‘industry standard’ I’d say but I’ve never charged for samples.
[09/04/2026, 21:23:46] Tim Dunlop: I have used these loads

[17/04/2026, 12:06:53] ~ Dan: This is the area that I struggle the most to some degree. There's a massive element of you don't know what you don't know. 

I have had some success so far using various prompt generators to help shape the prompt first by asking it to create a prompt for that specific problem including as much detail and the sort of breakdown you'd like. 

Ultimately with any LLM you'll get out what you put in, so making the input as structured and readable to a machine as possible will hopefully get the results you need
